{"code":1,"isDone":false,"toInsertArticleList":[{"createdTime":"2021-09-28 00:55:28","updatedTime":"2021-09-28 00:55:28","title":"German election: Greens poised to join government of Europe's biggest economy after voters reject Merkel's party","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/germany-green-party-government-europe-biggest-economy-elections-scholz-2021-9","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/6151b6a12fb46b0019be2f94?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Olaf Scholz, wearing a suit and carrying a bouquet of flowers smiles after his party, the SPD, gathered the most votes in Germany&#39;s preliminary elections  results\" height=\"1000\" width=\"2000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Olaf Scholz, chancellor candidate for the SPD, September 27 in Berlin, Germany.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Florian Gaertner/Photothek/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Germany&#39;s green party is likely to be a part of the next coalition leading the country. </li>\n<li>Preliminary results put them in a strong position to push its anti-climate-change policies.</li>\n<li>Either the leading SPD or the incumbent CDU could seek a coalition with the Greens. </li>\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>Germany&#39;s green party could soon be part of the government in Europe&#39;s biggest economy, with results from Sunday&#39;s election giving them a crucial role in upcoming coalition-building. </p><p>As of Tuesday, Germany&#39;s Social Democrats (SPD), a center-left group, had gained 25.7% of the votes in the election, <a href=\"https://www.bundeswahlleiter.de/en/bundeswahlleiter.html\">according to preliminary results</a>.</p><p>It put them ahead of Angela Merkel&#39;s Christian Democrats (CDU), who after 16 years in power were left with just 18.9% of the votes as of Monday afternoon local time.</p><p>The Greens (&#34;Die Grünen&#34; in German) came with 14.8%, enough to give it a powerful role in the coalition-forming that characterizes the formation of German governments.</p><p>&#34;Voters have clearly spoken,&#34; said Olaf Scholz, the Social Democrats&#39; candidate for chancellor, per <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/live/2021/09/26/world/german-elections\">The New York Times</a>.</p><p>Shortly after he announced that his first choice for a coalition would be a so-called &#34;traffic light&#34; arrangement.</p><p>Under this, his SPD would join with the Greens and also the free-market FDP, which has an 11.5% vote share.</p><p>A so-called traffic light coalition would bring the SPD (red), the FDP (yellow), and the Greens together to push their occupation of seats in parliament over the majority. Together they have a 52% vote share, per the provisional results.</p><p>Coalition forming can take a long time - Scholz on Monday said he hoped to have a coalition together by Christmas.</p><p>It is also possible that the CDU tries to form a less stable coalition with the Greens and FDP.</p><p>Either approach puts the Greens in a good place to push their anti-climate-change and progressive policies, <a href=\"https://www.dw.com/en/what-do-the-german-greens-want-if-they-gain-power/a-57248907\">according to Germany&#39;s public broadcaster Deutsche Welle</a>.</p><p>&#34;The next government has to be a climate government. We will do all we can to achieve this,&#34; the Greens&#39; chancellor candidate Annalena Baerbock <a href=\"https://twitter.com/ABaerbock/status/1442191652177678336?s=20\">said in a tweet on Monday</a>. </p><p>The Greens are close to the SPD. They were part of an SPD coalition that began in the later 1990s under chancellor Gerhard Schroeder. The parties have &#34;different objectives&#34; but there is &#34;a lot of overlaps&#34; in specific policy areas, Scholz previously said, <a href=\"https://www.politico.eu/article/olaf-scholz-spd-green-alliance-germany-election-2021/\">Politico reported</a>.</p><p>Among the policies that are espoused by both are raising the national minimum wage to €12 ($14) per hour, increasing renewable energies, and making Germany a hub for the development of electric vehicles, per Politico.</p><p>The Green party has promised to cut greenhouse gases by 70% by 2030, significantly more aggressive than existing targets of 55% by that date, per Deutsche Welle. </p><p>The two parties might disagree in some areas of foreign policy, especially when it comes to the country&#39;s approach to Russia and China.</p><p>The Greens are likely to be staunchly opposed to cooperation on the grounds of human-rights abuses in Hong Kong and Xinjiang, <a href=\"https://www.ft.com/content/5b7b9604-0f79-4ce8-8116-7b13e03b8741\">per the Financial Times</a>. </p><p>The SPD, however, faces an uphill battle with the FDP leader, which has previously said that the party had little in common with the other two parties, <a href=\"https://www.reuters.com/world/europe/german-traffic-light-coalition-seen-most-likely-2021-09-27/\">per Reuters</a>. </p><p>The FDP&#39;s leader, Christian Lindner, announced on Monday he and FDP General Secretary Volker Wissing were in preliminary talks with the Greens, <a href=\"https://www.welt.de/politik/bundestagswahl/article234053742/Bundestagswahl-FDP-Chef-Lindner-kuendigt-Vorsondierungen-mit-den-Gruenen-an.html\">per Die Welt. </a></p><div 00:55:29","updatedTime":"2021-09-28 00:55:29","title":"The NYC subway is so desperate for more train operators that it's reportedly offering retired conductors up to $35,000 for 90 days of work","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/nyc-mta-train-operator-shortage-offering-up-to-35k-retirees-2021-9","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/6151eb672fb46b0019be3f19?format=jpeg\" alt=\"mta new york city subway conductor\" height=\"504\" width=\"1009\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>The MTA is pushing for another round of emergency federal aid to fund its subways, buses, and commuter trains.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Alexi Rosenfeld/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>The New York subway system is suffering from a severe shortage of train operators.</li>\n<li>More than one in 10 rush hour trains were cancelled in August - the worst rate since at least 2015.</li>\n<li>Transit officials are offering retirees up to $35,000 to work for three months, per the NY Daily News.</li>\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>Part of the US labor market&#39;s difficulties can be attributed to the COVID-19 pandemic motivating older workers to retire earlier than planned.</p><p>At the New York Metropolitan Transportation Authority, which employs more than 66,000 people, that rush to the exits has left the agency with about 5,000 fewer workers since the start of the pandemic, <a href=\"https://www.nydailynews.com/new-york/ny-mta-retirees-subway-shortage-20210926-sbevdxwfhfgvxjfezeerzx5oqm-story.html\">the New York Daily News reported</a>.</p><p>Of those 5,000 retirees, about one in five were in subway services, and that has led to severe complications for the transit system. In August more than 10% of rush-hour trains were cancelled, the worst performance since at least 2015.</p><p>Now transit officials are offering a sizable pay package to attract retired train operators who have left the MTA in the last three years, according to a letter obtained by the Daily News.</p><p>In January 2019, there were 3,598 train operators, according to <a href=\"https://www.thecity.nyc/2021/4/18/22390464/nyc-subway-workforce-dwindles-as-pandemic-takes-toll\">data reported by TheCity.nyc</a>, but that figure had dropped 11.5% to 3,182 as of February 2021.</p><p>&#34;New York City Transit has a critical and urgent need for fully trained and qualified train operators,&#34; an MTA human resources officer wrote in the letter. &#34;If you choose to apply and are accepted, you will be temporarily appointed by NYC Transit for a period not exceeding 90 days and your gross earnings, which are not pensionable, may not exceed $35,000.&#34;</p><p>The average NYC Transit worker earned annual compensation of $75,891 in 2015, according to a report from the <a href=\"https://ibo.nyc.ny.us/iboreports/printnycbtn65.pdf\">city&#39;s budget office</a>.</p><p>An additional challenge facing the system is an upcoming deadline for employees to prove their vaccination status or submit to weekly testing in order to continue working.</p><p><a href=\"https://www.nydailynews.com/new-york/ny-mta-vaccine-mandate-union-leaders-20210913-sp7ffe6xzve27etmfssb3xcsdq-story.html\">The Daily News previously reported</a> that 58% of MTA workers have gotten at least one shot of the COVID-19 vaccine, but union representatives say that many unvaccinated members would quit their jobs instead of get inoculated. At least 171 workers have died of COVID-19.</p><bi-shortcode id=\"expanded-coverage\" class=\"mceNonEditable\" data-source=\"what-is-the-labor-shortage-and-how-long-will-it-last\" (Dominick Reuter)","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i.insider.com/6151eb672fb46b0019be3f19?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn72@2020_3/2021/09/27/16-55-34-964_5659e3ba6bef898e.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn72@2020_4/2021/09/27/16-55-37-727_cc3ef43fc8ff5801.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=pRiE7IrzfFA:nfdVqMzMxCM:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn52@2020_4/2021/09/27/16-55-37-608_b0a8c7c6d4398e63.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=pRiE7IrzfFA:nfdVqMzMxCM:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn31@2020_6/2021/09/27/16-55-32-700_8da00e6cbc5c479c.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn51@2020_3/2021/09/27/16-55-35-450_740defb435ea442b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=pRiE7IrzfFA:nfdVqMzMxCM:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn44@2020_1/2021/09/27/16-55-37-603_946b170194d81d02.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/businessinsider/~4/pRiE7IrzfFA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn88@2020_4/2021/09/27/16-55-35-663_009fb85104284a04.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1632761729138},{"createdTime":"2021-09-28 00:55:28","updatedTime":"2021-09-28 00:55:28","title":"McConnell's Senate GOP is about to vote for a US default. Here's what the debt ceiling is - and why it's so dangerous.","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/what-is-debt-ceiling-explained-democrats-republicans-government-spending-borrowing-2021-9","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/6143a6b32db0850019a9e136?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Mitch McConnell\" height=\"2109\" width=\"4218\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Senate Minority Leader Mitch McConnell of Kentucky.\n<p class=\"copyright\">AP/Andrew Harnik</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Congress is sparring again over raising the debt ceiling. The economy&#39;s fate hangs in the balance.</li>\n<li>The ceiling caps how much the government can borrow, but it&#39;s turned into a political weapon.</li>\n<li>Here&#39;s what the debt ceiling is and why it&#39;s so dangerous for the US economy.</li>\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>Senate Republicans are on the precipice of voting for America to default on its debt, a step that could eventually plunge the US into recession.</p><p>Senate Minority Leader Mitch McConnell is spearheading the effort to reject a measure <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/house-approves-bill-government-shutdown-default-gop-set-to-oppose-2021-9\">that cleared the House</a> along party-lines last week to raise what&#39;s known as the debt ceiling. They want Democrats to address it on their own, and the Democrats reject that because both parties racked up the federal debt.</p><p>If that sounds familiar, you already know a fair deal about the &#34;debt ceiling.&#34; If it doesn&#39;t, you don&#39;t know that every so often, Congress has to ask itself permission to pay America&#39;s bills, and roughly half of it usually doesn&#39;t want to. That&#39;s the &#34;ceiling&#34; that has to be raised.</p><p>In other words, each party is waiting for the other to blink first.</p><p>Experts say a federal default could cause upheaval that reverberates through the economy <a href=\"https://twitter.com/josephzeballos/status/1439969190413230087?s=20\">in the form</a> of job losses, abrupt cuts to Social Security payments, and American troops missing out on scheduled paychecks. </p><h2>Behind America&#39;s ever-growing debt pile</h2><p>The federal government relies on debt. It regularly spends more than it raises in taxes. To cover that cost, the Treasury Department borrows cash by auctioning bonds. That&#39;s why the government&#39;s debt pile exceeds $28 trillion.</p><p>The debt ceiling was introduced in 1917 to encourage the government to slow its borrowing. Reaching the limit forces one of two outcomes. Congress could raise the ceiling or temporarily suspend it and punt the problem into the future. Or the government could default on its debt.</p><p>Over the past 50 years, the government has raised it 57 times. It&#39;s not hard to understand why. Defaulting on government debt could drag the country into an economic recession. Spending on critical programs would freeze. The dollar&#39;s strength would likely plummet. And financial markets would slide into uncharted territory as investors brace for an unprecedented economic catastrophe.</p><p>There&#39;s an even greater risk. The dollar is the world&#39;s reserve currency, meaning other governments depend on it holding its worth. A default on US government debt would dramatically shake that confidence. The dollar&#39;s value could plummet as the world financial system prices in a dollar that isn&#39;t reliable - or seeks an alternative.</p><p>The US has avoided this for as long as the debt ceiling has existed, but as it has steadily climbed higher, it&#39;s served more as a political weapon than a true threshold. Democrats and Republicans have both sparred over raising the ceiling, and their latest spat is no different.</p><h2>The latest battle over borrowing</h2><p>McConnell has repeatedly said Senate Republicans won&#39;t vote to lift the limit, adding on Monday that &#34;<a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/mcconnell-vote-default-debt-ceiling-government-funding-shutdown-pelosi-schumer-2021-9\">Democrats do not need our help</a>.&#34; It&#39;s reminiscent of a 2011 showdown between House Republicans and the Obama administration that prompted tumult in financial markets and led to the first downgrade of the nation&#39;s credit rating.</p><p>Other Republican lawmakers have said that raising the ceiling would open the door for Democrats to pass their $3.5 trillion spending plan.</p><p>House Speaker Nancy Pelosi and Senate Majority Leader Chuck Schumer have said Republicans are more to blame for the debt issue. Raising the debt limit is also about dealing with past spending both parties racked up during the Trump administration, they said.</p><p>Under President Donald Trump, GOP lawmakers drove the deficit sharply higher with their 2017 tax cuts and early COVID-19 rescue package by nearly $8 trillion. Lifting the limit, Democrats say, lets the government pay the bills for those policies.</p><p>Pelosi and Schumer hope to pass legislation that will fund the government through December, but that&#39;s perhaps a choice they shouldn&#39;t have to make.</p><p>Eliminating the limit is the &#34;<a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/congress-debt-ceiling-box-dynamite-recession-jpmorgan-treasury-biden-mcconnell-2021-9\">obvious solution</a>&#34; to avoiding periodic debt scares, David Kelly, the chief global strategist at JPMorgan Funds, said in a recent note. There&#39;s no evidence that the ceiling slows the growth of government debt, the very task it was created for in the first place. Instead of repeatedly voting for temporary fixes, Congress can do away with the limit and focus more on crafting a sustainable budget, Kelly said.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original src=\"https://i.insider.com/603d029087d37600190d05b7?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Polestar Space 002\" height=\"1000\" width=\"2000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>A Polestar Space showroom.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Polestar</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Swedish EV maker Polestar will go public in a $20 billion deal with special purpose acquisition company Gores Guggenheim. </li>\n<li>Polestar makes high-performance vehicles and was founded by Volvo Cars and China&#39;s Zhejiang Geely Holding. </li>\n<li>The deal should close in the first half of 2022 and would bring Polestar to trade on the Nasdaq. </li>\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>Polestar is preparing to go public through blank-check company Gores Guggenheim in a deal that values the Swedish electric vehicle maker at about $20 billion, the companies jointly said Monday. <br><br>The high-performance automaker was founded in 2017 by Volvo Cars and Chinese automaker <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/stocks/gelyf-stock\">Zhejiang Geely Holding</a>, known as Geely, and counts Geely Chairman Eric Li and actor Leonardo DiCaprio among its investors. It&#39;s<a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/how-volvo-ev-polestar-take-on-tesla-north-america-2021-1?inline-read-more\"> been positioning itself</a> to rival <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/stocks/tsla-stock\">Tesla</a> and <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/stocks/lcid-stock\">Lucid</a>.  <br><br>Current Polestar stakeholders will retain roughly 94% ownership in the company and roll 100% of their equity interests into the pro forma company. The SPAC deal implies an enterprise value of about $20 billion for the combined company, representing about 3.0 times estimated revenue for 2023, and 1.5x 2024 estimated revenue.  <br><br>The newly formed company would be called Polestar Automotive Holding UK Ltd. and is expected to be listed on the Nasdaq under the ticker symbol &#34;PSNY&#34;.</p><p>The boards of Polestar and Gores Guggenheim have unanimously approved the deal, which is expected to close in the first half of 2022, subject to approval by Gores Guggenheim&#39;s stockholders and other closing conditions.</p><p>The company&#39;s Polestar 1 and Polestar 2 electric cars are already on roads in Europe, North America, and Asia. Deliveries reached about 10,000 vehicles in 2020, and management projects selling 290,000 vehicles a year by 2025. </p><p>Polestar plans to launch the premium electric SUV Polestar 3 in 2022, and introduce two more models by 2024 as it aims to expand global distribution to 30 markets by 2023. </p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a 00:55:28","updatedTime":"2021-09-28 00:55:28","title":"Walmart does away with layaway, which didn't carry any fees, and announces buy now, pay later program that may charge customers interest","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/walmart-layaway-canceled-affirm-finance-buy-now-pay-later-2021-9","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/614f7299b414c10018627116?format=jpeg\" alt=\"walmart cashier\" height=\"2622\" width=\"3496\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Walmart cashier Regina Tommy waits on customers.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Jonathan Newton / The Washington Post via Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Walmart decided to get rid of layaway before the 2021 holiday season.</li>\n<li>The company announced it&#39;s using Affirm, a buy now, pay later service the retailer partnered with in 2019.</li>\n<li>Walmart started to phase its layaway out last year, only allowing it for select jewelry purchases. </li>\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/typical-walmart-shopper-demographic-white-woman-earning-middle-income-2021-7\">Walmart has decided to scrap its layaway program</a> completely before the 2021 holiday season, replacing it with a <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/buy-now-pay-later-bnpl-threats-payments-merchants-fees-data-2021-8\">buy now, pay later</a> financing option. </p><p>The retailer is now using the company Affirm, which <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/walmart-affirm-point-of-sale-financing-2019-2\">partnered with Walmart in 2019</a>, to replace layaway. Instead of having stores hold items from late August through mid-December while customers make payments until paid in full, shoppers can now take the item home immediately and pay it off with Affirm.</p><p>Unlike layaway, purchases made with Affirm may charge customers interest. Although Affirm does not charge any hidden or late fees for using its services, customers can have an APR rate on purchases of 10-30% depending on their credit and 0% for select promotional items on Walmart.com.</p><p>Not all Walmart customers may be eligible to use Affirm depending on their prequalification status. The service can be used on purchases ranging from $144 to $2,000 and excludes items like alcohol, groceries and food, personal care products, and pet supplies.</p><p>Last year, <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/walmart-hiring-spree-supply-chain-workers-holidays-2021-9\">Walmart, the world&#39;s largest retailer,</a> started to phase out layaway, only offering it on select jewelry items. </p><p>&#34;We&#39;ve learned a lot in the past year as our customers&#39; needs and shopping habits have changed,&#34; a Walmart spokesperson told Insider. &#34; We are confident that our payment options provide the right solutions for our customers.&#34;</p><p>Affirm&#39;s services operate alternatively to a credit card. Customers will purchase the item immediately, and pay for the items over a three to 24 month period. Customers can select their own payment plan and Affirm will match them with a lender who will provide them with a loan for the financed item.</p><p>Walmart shoppers can return any purchases made with Affirm for a refund, but the amount they paid in interest will not be refunded. Partial payments or late payments may impact a consumer&#39;s credit score or ability to receive new loans with the company, according to Affirm.</p><p>Some Walmart customers expressed concern on social media, worried about how families would be able to purchase gifts for the holiday season if they don&#39;t qualify for Affirm.</p><blockquote class=\"twitter-tweet blockquote\" data-lang=\"en\" data-cards=\"\" data-conversation=\"\">-THEE STEP MUVA  (@Stepmuvalex) <a href=\"https://twitter.com/mims/statuses/1441782692455010306?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw\">September 25, 2021</a></blockquote><script async=\"\" src=\"//platform.twitter.com/widgets.js\" charset=\"utf-8\"></script><p>&#34;Walmart took away layaway and replaced it with Affirm, which checks credit… right before Christmas,&#34; one Twitter user wrote. &#34;So many low income families are not gonna be able to give their children gifts.&#34;</p><p>Some customers seemed to not mind the change, making jokes that without layaway they have no place to hide their children&#39;s gifts during the holidays.</p><p>Walmart has recently undergone several company-wide changes, including raising its <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/15-minimum-wage-not-enough-retail-workers-2021-9\">minimum wage</a>, getting rid of its <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/walmart-scrap-quarterly-bonuses-for-store-workers-wage-salary-pay-2021-9\">employee bonus program</a> for hourly workers, and opening <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/what-its-like-to-visit-ghost-kitchens-walmart-location-in-rochester-new-york-photos-review-2021-9\">Ghost Kitchens</a> inside select stores. </p><div Walsh)","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i.insider.com/614f7299b414c10018627116?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn8@2020_3/2021/09/27/16-55-35-239_960b0edee06806e6.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn72@2020_4/2021/09/27/16-55-37-727_cc3ef43fc8ff5801.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=C1USLN85pOg:hw4H2VoxDJM:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn95@2020_2/2021/09/27/16-55-33-141_14ae9b4e436c087b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=C1USLN85pOg:hw4H2VoxDJM:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn27@2020_4/2021/09/27/16-55-38-499_819110e19f072b2e.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn51@2020_3/2021/09/27/16-55-35-450_740defb435ea442b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=C1USLN85pOg:hw4H2VoxDJM:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn51@2020_1/2021/09/27/16-55-33-099_e831cd0c35594532.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/businessinsider/~4/C1USLN85pOg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn7@2020_2/2021/09/27/16-55-35-757_8c64bcfbb068912f.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1632761728355},{"createdTime":"2021-09-28 00:55:28","updatedTime":"2021-09-28 00:55:28","title":"Key Senate Democrat unveils plan to make states provide at least 26 weeks of unemployment benefits","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/ron-wyden-senate-democrat-unemployment-benefits-congress-2021-9","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/6076f374420615001817562e?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Ron Wyden\" height=\"1000\" width=\"2000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Sen. Ron Wyden (D-OR) asks questions to a panel of pharmaceutical company CEOs during a hearing held by the Senate Finance Committee on &#34;Drug Pricing in America: A Prescription for Change, Part II&#34; February 26, 2019 in Washington, DC. The committee heard testimony from a panel of pharmaceutical company CEOs on the reasons for rising costs of prescription drugs.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Photo by Win McNamee/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Wyden is introducing a bill to mandate states provide at least 26 weeks of benefits.</li>\n<li>&#34;This system has been intentionally broken to minimize the numbers of jobless workers who can access it,&#34; he said in a statement.</li>\n<li>The Oregon Democrat wants to include the measure in a $3.5 trillion spending plan.</li>\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>Sen. Ron Wyden of Oregon, chair of the Senate Finance Committee, unveiled a new bill on Monday to compel states to provide at least 26 weeks of unemployment benefits, a move aimed at establishing a uniform standard for jobless Americans.</p><p>The legislation is an attempt to forge a national standard out of the country&#39;s patchwork of state unemployment systems, which vary greatly from one state to the next. For example, Florida residents qualify for up to 19 weeks of jobless payments, but Alabama residents can only get unemployment checks for 14 weeks, <a href=\"https://www.cbpp.org/research/economy/how-many-weeks-of-unemployment-compensation-are-available\">per the Center on Budget and Policy Priorities.</a></p><p>&#34;This proposal makes a down payment on long-overdue reform to our unemployment system, and was designed to fit in our upcoming package,&#34; Wyden said in a statement to Insider. &#34;This system has been intentionally broken to minimize the numbers of jobless workers who can access it, and we&#39;re going take significant steps toward fixing it.&#34; </p><p>The measure is also backed by Sens. Sherrod Brown of Ohio and Michael Bennet of Colorado. Brown heads the Senate Banking Committee. </p><p>Wyden said the plan would ensure six months of benefits and coverage for part-time workers and improve the administration of aging state unemployment programs. The bill would compel state online filing systems to be available around the clock and be accessible for non-English speakers, among other fixes. It also intends to prevent states from cutting the length of unemployment benefits, <a href=\"https://www.cbpp.org/blog/states-cutting-jobless-benefits-hadnt-adequately-prepared-for-recession\">as many did</a> after the Great Recession.</p><p>&#34;Senator Wyden&#39;s bill offers Democrats a way to make a down payment on long overdue fixes to the unemployment system,&#34; Andrew Stettner, a senior fellow and jobless-policy expert at the left-leaning Century Foundation, said in a statement to Insider.</p><p>Bennet, Brown, and Wyden want the measure to form part of <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/whats-in-democrats-35-trillion-infrastructure-economic-plan-2021-9\">the Democratic $3.5 trillion spending plan</a> that&#39;s moving through Congress. It is not clear whether the provisions will garner unanimous support from all 50 Senate Democrats needed to clear the party-line reconciliation process.</p><p>Wyden has long pushed to overhaul how states manage their unemployment programs after many melted down at the onset of the pandemic. Millions of out-of-work Americans <a href=\"https://www.epi.org/blog/unemployment-filing-failures-new-survey-confirms-that-millions-of-jobless-were-unable-to-file-an-unemployment-insurance-claim/\">reported delays</a> in receiving checks, with many jamming phone lines or lining up at state offices in frantic efforts to secure government assistance.</p><p>Last year, Congress approved several measures to beef up unemployment checks, with $600 per week from March 2020 through July 2020. After a lapse, lawmakers authorized $300 weekly federal benefits from December 2020 until September 6, along with extending eligibility to gig workers and long-term unemployed people. </p><p>Those federal initiatives <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/unemployment-benefits-system-broken-pandemic-congress-2021-9\">expired</a> on Labor Day, <a href=\"https://twitter.com/pelhamprog/status/1441026454146453510?s=20\">cutting off at least 8.5 million people</a> from all jobless aid.</p><p>&#34;After abruptly ending pandemic extended benefits amidst the Delta surge, the least Congress can do is make sure jobless workers get at least 26 weeks of benefits regardless of where they live and lose their job,&#34; Stettner said.</p><div Avast.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Avast</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Insider spoke with more than 20 female executives of color working at the top of corporate Europe.</li>\n<li>Some shared experiences of racism or sexism in the workplace.</li>\n<li>They describe what happened and what they learned from these experiences.</li>\n</ul>\n<ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>When Insider spoke to <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/women-of-color-ceos-and-executives-in-corporate-europe-2021-9?r=US&IR=TIR=T\">more than 20 female CEOs and executives of color about the challenges they faced climbing the corporate ladder</a>, some recounted confrontations with colleagues, and said there were times they wished they&#39;d spoken out about the sexism and racism they faced at work.</p><p>Mohanna Azarmandi, chief learning officer at Microsoft Germany, described a meeting in which she disagreed with a male peer who was blaming others on the team for mistakes. He responded, she said, by calling her &#34;young lady&#34; and suggested she &#34;let the grown-ups talk.&#34;</p><p><em><strong>Read more: </strong><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/women-of-color-ceos-and-executives-in-corporate-europe-2021-9?\">21 powerful female CEOs and executives of color reflect on what they&#39;ve learned from climbing Europe&#39;s corporate ladder</a></em></p><p>Azarmandi said she kept her cool and replied: &#34;I don&#39;t appreciate being talked down to.&#34; She said she told her colleague that everyone was &#34;eye-to-eye on this project, each of us has to represent our departments,&#34; and said she would &#34;appreciate him being respectful with me and everyone else in the room.&#34;</p><p>Azarmandi said: &#34;This person never talked down to me ever again and kept asking for me and my team in future projects.&#34;</p><p>Her experience is one of several recounted by the inspiring women Insider spoke with, from companies including Facebook, PwC, Tiktok, Unilever, and DeepMind.</p><p>Jaya Baloo, chief information security officer of Avast, described a meeting in which a colleague made a comment about &#34;these brown people&#34; while discussing operations in India.</p><p>She said the eyes in the room fell on her but it took her &#34;at least a minute&#34; to realize.</p><p>&#34;It was hard to address the pity that was coming from the people in the room&#34;, she said. &#34;I constantly forget that this is a challenge, that I am female and have this skin color, even that I&#39;m short and that I won&#39;t be taken seriously or shown a different degree of empathy because of it.&#34;</p><p>She added: &#34;I don&#39;t see the benefit of boxing myself in so there&#39;s absolutely no good reason to do that to anyone else.&#34;</p><p>Dr. Navina Evans, CEO of Health Education England, said she was &#34;quite timid&#34; when she started out and wished she&#39;d had the vocabulary to speak out about racist and sexist treatment. </p><p>&#34;I wish I&#39;d had the confidence and ability to influence people to act and think differently without alienating them back then,&#34; she said. &#34;But because I didn&#39;t know how, I was often silent.</p><p>&#34;So, the lesson is to prepare others so that they will not be silent.&#34;</p><div Fields)","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i.insider.com/614dd8e02a37a70018027b75?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn8@2020_3/2021/09/27/16-55-34-674_6bb0a34dcca23dff.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn72@2020_4/2021/09/27/16-55-37-727_cc3ef43fc8ff5801.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=qWw0NoVQlRo:3JdV7S0x_jQ:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn88@2020_4/2021/09/27/16-55-34-144_f863bc2904f7c632.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=qWw0NoVQlRo:3JdV7S0x_jQ:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn4@2020_5/2021/09/27/16-55-36-173_ac8d27b3320eee8d.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn51@2020_3/2021/09/27/16-55-35-450_740defb435ea442b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=qWw0NoVQlRo:3JdV7S0x_jQ:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn55@2020_3/2021/09/27/16-55-37-647_61a58f3b1f426c53.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/businessinsider/~4/qWw0NoVQlRo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn48@2020_6/2021/09/27/16-55-33-936_46d0cc5099b3afb6.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1632761728373},{"createdTime":"2021-09-28 00:55:28","updatedTime":"2021-09-28 00:55:28","title":"Tens of thousands of shipping containers are stuck off the coast of Southern California as the ports operate below capacity, combatting a crushing shortages of workers, equipment, and time","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/shipping-containers-stuck-california-ports-combat-shortages-2021-9","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/6151ec052fb46b0019be3f42?format=jpeg\" alt=\"GettyImages 1298696833\" height=\"2302\" width=\"3069\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Ships sit off the coast of Seal Beach, CA, on Tuesday, January 26, 2021. Cargo ships enduring one of the worst U.S. port bottlenecks in more than a decade faced down another obstacle as they waited to offload near the ports of Los Angeles and Long Beach\n<p class=\"copyright\"><a href=\"https://www.gettyimages.com/detail/news-photo/cargo-ship-sits-off-the-coast-of-san-pedro-on-tuesday-news-photo/1298696833?adppopup=true\" target=\"_blank\">Brittany Murray/MediaNews Group/Long Beach Press-Telegram via Getty Images</a></p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Key US ports in Southern California are facing near-record backlogs of cargo ships.</li>\n<li>The Port of Long Beach has moved to increase operating hours, which may not be enough to fix the issue.</li>\n<li>Shortages of workers and equipment, and a lack of coordination across the transportation industry have created a ripple effect.</li>\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>The largest port in the U.S. faces<a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/largest-us-port-breaks-multiple-record-cargo-ships-import-delays-2021-9\"> a near-record backlog of cargo ships</a>, and there&#39;s no end in sight.</p><p>Los Angeles and Long Beach ports had 62 cargo ships waiting to dock and unload as of Friday - a stark contrast to an average of one to zero ships before the pandemic. Today, ships at the port can wait for as long as three weeks, <a href=\"https://volumes.portoptimizer.com/\">Port LA data shows.</a></p><p>Despite the historic backup, the ports are only operating at 60% to 70% capacity, Uffe Ostergaard, president of the North America region for German ship operator Hapag Lloyd told <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/reviews/out?u=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.wsj.com%2Farticles%2Fcargo-delays-are-getting-worse-but-california-ports-still-rest-on-weekends-11632648602\">The Wall Street Journal.</a></p><p>&#34;That&#39;s a huge operational disadvantage,&#34; Ostergaard said, pointing to the fact that the two ports are closed for several hours most days, as well as on Sundays - making it more difficult to keep pace with the ports in Asia and Europe that are sending the goods on a 24/7 schedule.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/6151f02b2fb46b0019be4088?format=jpeg\" alt=\"A container ship docked at Mombasa port, Kenya, with loading cranes in view.\" height=\"1333\" width=\"1777\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>A container ship docked next to cranes at the port of Mombasa, Kenya, in October 2019.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Baz Ratner/Reuters</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>Last week, the Port of Long Beach <a href=\"https://www.freightwaves.com/news/lalb-ports-to-test-expanded-night-weekend-hours\">moved to increase their hours of operation</a> to 24-hours on Monday through Thursday. The Port of Los Angeles did not follow suit, choosing instead to maintain its existing hours. </p><p>The traditional routine at the ports includes two shifts for longshore workers: 8 a. to 4 pm and 6 pm to 3 am. The ports are closed on Sundays. Overnight shifts and Saturdays are more expensive and rarely used, The Journal reported. </p><h2>Longer hours may not be enough.</h2><p>Gene Seroka, executive director of Port Los Angeles, said longer hours do little to address the backlog when truckers and warehouse operators have not similarly extended their hours. It&#39;s not optimal for truckers to pick up loads at night, especially when they&#39;d have to find alternative places to store the goods when the warehouses are not open at night.</p><p>What&#39;s more, many warehouses near the West Coast don&#39;t have space for the goods. About 98% of warehouses in Southern California&#39;s logistics-heavy Inland Empire region are fully occupied, while the entire Western U.S. has a 3.6% vacancy rate, Jason Tolliver, an executive at real-estate firm <a href=\"https://www.wsj.com/market-data/quotes/CWK\">Cushman &#38; Wakefield</a> told The Journal.</p><p>&#34;It has been nearly impossible to get everyone on the same page towards 24/7 operations,&#34; Seroka said.</p><h2>Shortages of workers and equipment exacerbate delays</h2><p>A <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/restaurant-prices-labor-shortage-worker-wages-food-2021-6\">struggle to hire enough workers</a> has had a tremendous impact on the transportation industry nationwide, causing headaches at ports, warehouses, railways, and trucking. Many companies have fewer workers than before the pandemic but face significantly <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/graphics-highlight-record-backlog-of-cargo-ships-in-california-2021-9\">more work</a> due to the boom in demand for goods since the pandemic started.</p><p>The shipping delays have made it more difficult for truckers to meet their deadlines and stay on schedule when it comes to picking up goods at ports. </p><p>The backlog has also caused a shortage of containers and the chassis needed to haul them. Containers wait for extended periods in ports, and it takes about twice as much time for operators to return the chassis, the Journal said.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/61420e042db0850019a98db9?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Port workers\" height=\"2000\" width=\"2666\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>\n<p class=\"copyright\">Associated Press</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>Some cargo companies have even taken to storing their goods in the containers due to the lack of space at warehouses - as shipping containers represent a cheaper option than renting storage space.  Last week, <a href=\"https://www.flexport.com/market-updates/freight-market-update-september-21-2021/\">Flexport said</a> shipments between Asia and North America were facing &#34;critical undercapacity&#34; when it came to available equipment.</p><p>The U.S. is not the only country struggling to keep up with a build-up of cargo ships. On Sunday, <a href=\"https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2021-09-26/from-nairobi-to-ningbo-see-the-supply-shocks-spanning-globe?srnd=premium&sref=LspfQlRvQlRv\">Bloomberg reported</a> that COVID-19 shutdowns had created a ripple effect, pushing the prices of goods across the globe higher.</p><p>The supply chain snarls are expected to create major issues for holiday shoppers. Executives have warned the <a href=\"https://www.9news.com.au/national/shipping-delays-global-supply-chain-mess-will-end-2023/94548dcc-87d0-4993-87a9-b0dde563380d\">shipping crisis will continue into 2023.</a></p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a href='https://www.businessinsider.com/shipping-containers-stuck-california-ports-combat-shortages-2021-9'>Business lower Monday as investors entered the last week of September with lawmakers in Washington facing funding deadlines to avoid a government shutdown while worries lingered over China&#39;s debt-laden Evergrande. </p><p>The S&#38;P 500 and the Nasdaq Composite lost ground while the Dow Jones Industrial Average clung to a small gain. Tech stocks were feeling the pinch from investors seeking safety in the Treasury market, a move that pushed the 10-year Treasury yield to 1.48%, around its highest since late June. </p><p><strong>Here&#39;s where US indexes stood at 9:30 a.m. on Monday:  </strong></p><ul><li aria-level=\"1\"><a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/index/s&p_500?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingest?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingestedium=ingest\"><strong>S&#38;P 500</strong></a><strong>: </strong>4,447.21, down 0.19% </li></ul><ul><li aria-level=\"1\"><a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/index/dow_jones?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingest?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingestm=ingest\"><strong>Dow Jones Industrial Average</strong></a><strong>: </strong>34,849.52, up 0.15% (51.52 points)</li></ul><ul><li aria-level=\"1\"><a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/index/nasdaq_composite?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingest?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingestm=ingest\"><strong>Nasdaq Composite</strong></a><strong>: </strong>14,962.84, down 0.58%</li></ul><p>Investors appeared wary of risk-taking as Congress prepares to vote on Thursday to deal with the $28 trillion debt ceiling the country is bumping up against. In the House, Democrats are still wrestling over a major infrastructure bill and a $3.5 trillion spending bill for social and environmental programs.  </p><p>Meanwhile, Evergrande, whose $309 billion in liabilities rattled global markets last week, passed a deadline to pay $83.5 million in dollar-bond interest. That&#39;s left investors wondering whether they will have to absorb massive losses after a 30-day grace period, <a href=\"https://www.reuters.com/world/china/china-evergrande-bondholders-limbo-over-debt-resolution-2021-09-24/\">according to Reuters</a>. Another <a href=\"https://www.reuters.com/article/china-evergrande-debt-bonds-maturities-idUSL8N2QP1H5\">$47.5 million coupon</a> is due this week on a second dollar bond.</p><p><a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/commodities/gold-price?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingest?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingestm=ingest\">Gold</a> rose by 0.1% to $1,752.78 per ounce. </p><p>Oil prices jumped.<a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/commodities/oil-price?type=wti?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingest?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingestm=ingest\"> West Texas Intermediate crude</a> gained 2.1% to $75.55 per barrel.<a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/commodities/oil-price\"> Brent oil</a>, oil&#39;s international benchmark, picked up 1.8% to $79.46. </p><p><a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/currencies/btc-usd\">Bitcoin</a> gained about 1% at $43,545.91.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a +0000","feedId":22,"bgimg":"https://i.insider.com/613864702534ab0018533e73?format=jpeg","linkMd5":"4acc0c45e9c874f7b185d83df6e853ba","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn64@2020_1/2021/09/27/16-55-38-776_c57cb2d81f94ed47.webp","destWidth":5119,"destHeight":3840,"sourceBytes":732006,"destBytes":732006,"author":"insider@insider.com (Carla Mozée)","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i.insider.com/6151c56fb414c10018627e94?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn95@2020_5/2021/09/27/16-55-36-909_80c6dda8f0c3498a.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn72@2020_4/2021/09/27/16-55-37-727_cc3ef43fc8ff5801.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=_bA9P5tF8Ms:O0LVB0lfjxk:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn47@2020_1/2021/09/27/16-55-36-409_28a92f10c59f6018.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=_bA9P5tF8Ms:O0LVB0lfjxk:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn99@2020_3/2021/09/27/16-55-36-956_b36fd456d21513f0.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn51@2020_3/2021/09/27/16-55-35-450_740defb435ea442b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=_bA9P5tF8Ms:O0LVB0lfjxk:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn56@2020_4/2021/09/27/16-55-36-515_ce774aae0c7bc277.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/businessinsider/~4/_bA9P5tF8Ms":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn80@2020_3/2021/09/27/16-55-37-359_66cf31b9d675827b.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1632761728371},{"createdTime":"2021-09-28 00:55:28","updatedTime":"2021-09-28 00:55:28","title":"Visiting the gulag where my grandfather was tortured, but didn't officially exist","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/my-visit-to-the-gulag-where-my-grandfather-was-tortured-2021-9","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/614e3c0ab414c10018626454?format=jpeg\" alt=\"An older man walks away from a simple bridge, with the river in the background.\" height=\"2670\" width=\"4000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>A Belene survivor crosses the bridge across the Danube that connects the town of Belene and Persin island in 2015.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Dimitar Dilkoff/AFP via Getty Images)</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>This summer, Tana Ganeva traveled to Belene, Bulgaria&#39;s most notorious prison camp, where her grandfather was held in the 1950s.</li>\n<li>Bulgaria has effectively buried the history of its Communist-era gulags, where thousands were starved, tortured, and killed. </li>\n<li>Ganeva&#39;s grandfather attempted to escape Bulgaria four times, before making it to California. </li>\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>The island of Persin is a bird-watcher&#39;s paradise. Set on the Danube River, which divides Bulgaria and Romania, it&#39;s a nature park covered in wetlands and home to hundreds of rare bird species: the spoonbill, the pygmy cormorant, the corncrake, as well as herons, eagles, storks, and pelicans. </p><p>Amid the natural beauty, it&#39;s jarring to consider that this was the location of a concentration camp where thousands of Bulgarian political prisoners were brutalized and killed from 1949 to 1953 - and in some cases for years after that. Though it&#39;s officially known as Belene after the quiet Bulgarian village that sits 750 feet away on the mainland, old-timers here call it by another name: the Island of Death.</p><p>My stepgrandfather, Georgi Tutunjiev, was sent here at age 24 and spent four years and three months interred at Belene after someone (he suspected his ex-wife) told the authorities of his plan to escape the country. In his notebooks - he had planned to write a memoir about Belene but never did before he died in 2011 at 87 - he remembered the place as &#34;brutal facilities for re-education,&#34; where he&#39;d endured &#34;indescribable physical and psychological abuse.&#34; He finally managed to escape Bulgaria in 1966 and settle with my grandma in California. </p><p>In 1989, my parents and I left Bulgaria and joined my grandparents in California, thanks to the family-reunification policy. While many survivors of trauma shut down, my grandfather never <em>stopped</em> talking about the gulag. He seemed to have an unending loop of stories about Belene. For my immediate family, it could be exhausting, and we were alarmed to discover his extensive gun collection, which my grandmother gamely dismissed as a coping mechanism. But guests who came to the house were often riveted by his dark tales, which he mixed with his sense of humor. &#34;Jeko! <em>The Communistie</em> shot you!&#34; he&#39;d shout at his terrier mix, and the dog would sprawl on his back, playing dead.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/614b7386de0e7b00194bd3a3?format=jpeg\" alt=\"A green island is seen off the banks of a small town.\" height=\"1118\" width=\"1988\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>An aerial view of Persin island. The gulag was known as Belene, after the nearby town.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Tsvetomir Nikolaev</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>I&#39;ve come to the town of Belene on a brutally hot day in August for a tour of the Island of Death. I meet Nedyalka Toncheva, who works for the Belene Island Foundation, a nonprofit that organizes tours of the island, close to the bank of the Danube.</p><p>We cross a rickety water bridge on foot and then jump aboard a Jeep driven by a 24-year-old Belene native named Peter. Toncheva, who is 35, is passionate and knowledgeable about the island&#39;s flora and fauna. Every few minutes, she tells Peter to stop the car to point out a roosting stork or a water eagle. She talks about her plans to make Persin a tourist destination comparable to Borovets, a ski resort with luxury hotels in the Rila mountains; or Koprivchitsa, a living museum honoring the Bulgarian rebels who mounted an uprising in 1876 against the Ottoman Empire.</p><p>In the three decades since the fall of communism, Bulgaria has effectively buried the history of its many gulags, which operated mostly in the 1950s during the early, and most violent, days of Communist rule in the country<strong>.</strong> In Belene itself, many lower-level guards came from the village and a former mayor was also the gulag&#39;s first superintendent. It&#39;s not surprising that the village doesn&#39;t advertise its history.</p><p>After 1989, survivors who had been forced to sign documents promising to never talk about the camps started speaking out. For a brief time, they became the subjects of documentaries and newspaper profiles. But soon, the consensus was that it was better to move on. An interior minister tasked with investigating the camps instead secretly ordered a purge of thousands of pages of documents - 40% of the government record. </p><p>While Bulgaria&#39;s defeat of the Ottomans is central to the national identity, and much is made of the fact that <a href=\"https://www.washingtonpost.com/lifestyle/style/how-bulgaria-saved-its-jews-from-nazi-concentration-camps/2013/05/08/e866bdda-8cb1-11e2-9838-d62f083ba93f_story.html\">Bulgaria saved its Jews</a> during the Holocaust, the memory of the Communist era is more fraught. </p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/614ce14c4c42bc00190fa6b3?format=jpeg\" alt=\"A headshot of a man in a suit and tie.\" height=\"726\" width=\"592\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Georgi Tutunjiev, the author&#39;s grandfather, in around 1977.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Tana Ganeva</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>Peculiar for a tour, most of our stops lead us to what&#39;s <em>not</em> left of the camp. The shacks where prisoners slept have been razed - there&#39;s no trace of them.</p><p>At the entrance, in what is now an open field, an inscription says, &#34;To be human is to have dignity.&#34; From inside the camp - what would have been visible to the internees - the engraving says, &#34;If the enemy doesn&#39;t surrender, he is destroyed.&#34; But no one I&#39;ve talked to knows whether it&#39;s the original or has been recreated. </p><p>There are a few abandoned, falling-apart buildings, but those were built in 1959, six years after the camp&#39;s official (but not real) closing, when it was converted into a prison, in part to kill rumors that it had operated as a secret gulag. Todor Zhivkov, the Communist premier who took power in 1954 and stayed on until 1989, reopened it in the 1980s to detain Muslims who refused to take on Slavic names in place of their own - a disastrous bid to assimilate <a href=\"https://www.reuters.com/article/us-bulgaria-muslims/cracks-show-in-bulgarias-muslim-ethnic-model-idUSTRE55001C20090601\">them</a>. </p><p>I ask Toncheva whether there&#39;s a list of everyone who was held in the camp. I&#39;m thinking of my grandfather and wondering whether there&#39;s any documentation. She tells me everyone who comes here for the camp asks the same question.</p><p>&#34;There&#39;s no way to know, no list,&#34; Toncheva says, apologetic. &#34;There&#39;s almost no proof the camp even existed.&#34;</p><h2><strong>&#39;Perfectly calculated by Satan himself&#39;</strong></h2><p>The first contingent of 300 men arrived at the Belene camp in the summer of 1949, five years after the 1944 Communist coup. My grandfather, then 24, arrived that first winter. A camp for women was founded on an adjacent island soon after.</p><p>It was modeled after Josef Stalin&#39;s gulags in Siberia. Most of the prisoners had been dragged from their homes by the military police and sent here without trial. (Estimates vary, but 20,000 to 40,000 people were thought to be murdered by the Bulgarian Communist Party.) Even Stalin eventually warned <a href=\"http://etheses.lse.ac.uk/1524/1/U120848.pdf\">them</a> to scale down the killing of prominent oppositional figures or risk creating martyrs.</p><p>The first wave of prisoners had to hack through the unpopulated island and build small shacks that were so crowded the prisoners didn&#39;t have room to lie down. In his history of the camp, Borislav Skotchev wrote that the island was dotted with towers manned by guards with machine guns. </p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/614e3b01b414c100186263ab?format=jpeg\" alt=\"An older man holds a bouquet of flowers.\" height=\"2662\" width=\"4000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>A survivor of Belene during a commemoration ceremony in 2015.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Dimitar Dilkoff/AFP via Getty Images)</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>The men held here included the former leader of the Social Democrats, Orthodox priests (many in their 70s), and the mayor of Bulgaria&#39;s capital, Sofia. Tsveti Ivanov, the editor of the newspaper Svoboden Narod, or Free People, was sent to Belene after serving 10 months in prison. He was beaten so brutally that he got tetanus from his wounds and died in the compound. </p><p>Much of what we know about the place comes from survivors&#39; memoirs. They were fed a thin soup, sometimes with a handful of beans thrown in. Their bread ration - moldy or stale when it made its way to them - was small, and could be withheld by the guards as punishment. Sometimes they got tea. My grandfather told me that, in the winter, both the soup and the tea were given to them already frozen.</p><p>When Toncheva takes us on a brief walk to go look at storks, the ground gives off wet heat, and brambles and thorns claw at us, as if the island is alive and doesn&#39;t want us there. I think of the people who had to work days and nights, in sweltering summers, devoured by mosquitoes. It&#39;s unbelievable that anyone survived.</p><p>An internal CIA document described the grim situation of starving prisoners. &#34;A frequent sight is that of a prisoner eating raw green leaves and roots,&#34; it said. &#34;To be caught doing this, however, would result in 10 days in detention in a dungeon for such an offense.&#34; The lucky ones got packages from family, though those were often taken by guards. Many had little choice but to choke down the rotting carcasses of wild cats, killed and skinned for their fur by the villagers, or pick through horse dung for undigested barley. According to a CIA information report from March 13, 1952, during one brutal winter 30 prisoners died of cold or starvation.</p><p>&#34;It was an Inferno circle, perfectly calculated by Satan himself,&#34; Liliana Pirinchiva, one of the female survivors of Belene, wrote in her memoir. &#34;We were reduced to skeletons.&#34;</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/614b7479de0e7b00194bd42a?format=jpeg\" alt=\"A black-and-white photo of a group of people posing together.\" height=\"1245\" width=\"1780\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>A group of Bulgarian anarchists.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Tsvetana Dzhermanova</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>Then there were the guards, who brought an especially sadistic approach to their work. Some would chase packs of prisoners on horseback, letting their rifles off &#34;as if we were a flock of sheep,&#34; wrote Stefan Botchev, a survivor. When he got a severe case of scabies, the mites burrowing into his skin, he was locked up in a shed alone because the guards didn&#39;t want him to infect the cows. He recalled seeing a beating so severe that a prisoner&#39;s spine was broken, turning him into a &#34;reptile crawling on the ground.&#34;</p><p>Kouni Genchev Kounev, the chairman of the Bulgarian Youth Agrarian Union who also survived Belene, recalled one especially brutal punishment, in which the guards would pull back a prisoner&#39;s head and strike him in the trachea. They called it the &#34;sword stroke.&#34;</p><p>Years later, Krum Horozov, a survivor, would draw water colors of the camp from memory - it&#39;s virtually the only visual documentation that exists. In 2011, six years before his death, Horozov wrote: &#34;And when we die, which will be soon, who will remember what happened on that island in the 1950s, and will they know that people were sent there without a trial and sentence?&#34;</p><p>Lilia Topouzova, a historian in Toronto who writes about the history and the memory of the camps, recalls meeting Horozov at an academic conference; he was trying to give away copies of his drawings of Belene to university students, but they avoided him as if he were a pesky street vendor.</p><h2><strong>The Cricket</strong></h2><p>At 93, Tsvetana Dzhermanova is the last known survivor of the women&#39;s camp, which was known as Shturets, or Cricket. We&#39;re sitting outside her home in the mountain village of Leskovets, and she&#39;s talking so fast I wonder how she manages to breathe.</p><p>She smiles and laughs a lot, and she reminds me of my grandfather, who also spoke with the speed of a motorboat, frantic to tell his story.</p><p>&#34;I promised to outlive the Communistie<em>,</em> and here I am!&#34; she boasts. (My grandfather also took an understandable delight at outliving the Communistie. &#34;I survived the Communistie, but I won&#39;t survive old age,&#34; he once told me, when I was 25 and had no idea about either.)  </p><p><strong><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/614b73a9c2c9630018f5d4c1?format=jpeg\" alt=\"A crumpled, black and white portrait of a woman.\" height=\"1080\" width=\"824\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Tsvetana Dzhermanova.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Tsvetana Dzhermanova</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure></strong></p><p>Dzhermanova was an anarchist in the 1950s, and still is today. &#34;That&#39;s my personal ideology,&#34; she says. &#34;I&#39;m not sure humans are evolved enough to make either anarchism or socialism work the way they should, but for me, anarchism is it. Because I value freedom, family, friendship, and love.&#34;</p><p>When she first heard about anarchism as a teenager, she asked her mother what it meant. &#34;Anarchists are the people <em>all</em> regimes persecute,&#34; her mother had replied. That sold her. Dzhermanova joined a village group. She had no designs on power (detesting it) and mostly spent her time reading anarchist literature and working on a community vegetable garden. She estimates that 800 anarchists from the town were swept up in a night and sent to the gulags.</p><p>&#34;We sang songs while we worked,&#34; Dzhermanova tells me. &#34;That helped.&#34; </p><p>Last spring the sprightly nonagenarian made the three-hour trip to Belene to speak with a group of students about the camps. &#34;They had no idea about this. They were really surprised,&#34; she says. &#34;No one had ever talked to them about it, and they don&#39;t learn about it in school.&#34;</p><h2><strong>&#39;Out of Fashion&#39;</strong></h2><p>Toncheva and our driver, Peter, walk through a falling-down building that was constructed in 1959, in part to hide evidence of the camp. It&#39;s covered in bird shit. Plant life is taking over its rotted remnants, and old decayed furniture has been abandoned here and there. We talk about how nobody talks about the camp.</p><p>Peter tells us that despite having spent almost his entire life roughly 750 feet from Persin, in Belene village, he learned about the camp only two weeks earlier, when Toncheva hired him as a driver for her tours.</p><p>&#34;To think they only gave them bread and water, and made them work so hard,&#34; he says, shaking his head in disbelief.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/614e4296b414c10018626724?format=jpeg\" alt=\"A staircase is seen, with chipping paint.\" height=\"2000\" width=\"3000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>A crumbling building built on the site of Belene.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Stoyan Nenov/Reuters</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>As far as Toncheva knows, no one from her family was held here, but she remembers asking her grandmother about the island when she was a teenager and again after reading the memoirs of survivors. &#34;Shhh. Don&#39;t talk so much about this,&#34; her grandmother would say. &#34;You don&#39;t want to bring trouble.&#34;</p><p>There are rumors of a mass grave near Persin. Mikhail Mikailev, the head of the Belene Island Foundation, wants to find it. But money for the equipment required to find and dig up the remains eludes this two-person staff.</p><p>Unlike Peter and Toncheva, my parents, who were born in the mid-1950s and grew up in Bulgaria, tell me that in the 1970s and 1980s, all their friends in Sofia knew about Belene. &#34;We all heard the stories,&#34; my mother says.</p><p>But for the authorities, maintaining official denial was worth murder.</p><p>In 1969, the celebrated Bulgarian writer Georgi Markov defected to the West, where he wrote about the regime&#39;s abuses. In one essay, Markov described traveling on a boat down the Danube and approaching Belene. &#34;I remembered how, feet dangling over the edge of the boat, a youth with a guitar once sang a strange song: Danube, white river, how quiet you flow / Danube, black river, what anguish you know.&#34;</p><p><strong><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/614b7452c2c9630018f5d545?format=jpeg\" alt=\"A canoe is seen on calm waters at the banks of a lush stretch of land.\" height=\"1096\" width=\"1654\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>A view of Persin island.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Tsvetomir Nikolaev</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure></strong></p><p>On a rainy afternoon in London, a man <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2013/09/13/world/europe/bulgaria-prosecutor-closes-case-on-assassinated-defector.html\">jabbed the tip of his umbrella</a> into Markov&#39;s leg. Later, Markov noticed what looked like a small bug bite but didn&#39;t think much of it. A few days later he was dead, most likely poisoned by the Bulgarian secret service.</p><p>Before my visit to Belene, I met Topouzova, the historian, over Zoom to talk about the erasure of the camps in Bulgaria&#39;s consciousness. While former generals wrote best-sellers, the owner of a prominent bookstore dismissed any interest in survivors&#39; memoirs - they were &#34;out of fashion,&#34; he had told her.</p><p>It was gaslighting in its purest form. And it showed how we&#39;re all so prone to the &#34;just world&#34; fallacy, a phenomenon where if something is too horribly unjust, the human brain just kind of moves on. It&#39;s not all that hard to bury inconvenient truths.</p><p>&#34;It turned out that aging men and women with fragmented memories of bygone violence did not make for the faces of change,&#34; Topouzova wrote in a recent paper titled &#34;On Silence and History&#34; for the American Historical Association. &#34;The interned were rendered nonexistent - their experiences and memories fated to vanish along with the files.&#34; </p><h2><strong>A pile of stones</strong></h2><p>Nations define themselves by their monuments. The memorial in downtown Manhattan demands that we never forget the victims of 9/11. In the past few years, American activists have torn Confederate statues from their perches, signaling a break with the passive acceptance of the history of slavery. </p><p>Yet grappling with unpleasant history isn&#39;t easy. It was only in 2018 when a <a href=\"https://museumandmemorial.eji.org/memorial\">museum honoring the Black victims of lynching</a> opened in Alabama. The 1619 Project, which posits that the history of the United States is rooted in slavery, has spurred a massive backlash. School districts have <a href=\"https://www.newsweek.com/martin-luther-king-jr-rosa-parks-books-among-those-banned-penn-school-district-1630953\">banned</a> children&#39;s books about Rosa Parks. Vaunted democracies are as likely to try to bury inconvenient truths as former communist states.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/614e44542fb46b0019be19f9?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Three men face a wall of photos.\" height=\"2152\" width=\"3000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>At an exhibition in Sofia in 2009, Belene survivors look at images of the gulag&#39;s victims.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Stoyan Nenov/Reuters</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>In Bulgaria, there are monuments everywhere. From the smallest village to Sofia, the heroes of Bulgaria&#39;s uprising against the Ottoman Empire are eternalized in stone. In Plovdiv, a giant sculpture overlooks Bulgaria&#39;s second-largest city that honors &#34;Alyosha,&#34; an everyman Soviet soldier who helped &#34;liberate&#34; Bulgaria in the 1940s - even though many Bulgarians see that period as Soviet imperialism, much like the Ottoman Empire&#39;s 500 years of occupation.</p><p>The victims of Belene and the other camps have no such honor. The Belene foundation does the best it can. They helped organize an art exhibit, where Korozov&#39;s pencil drawings were tacked onto the walls of the decaying structures that had been erected to mask evidence of the gulag.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/614b7f90c2c9630018f5d96a?format=jpeg\" alt=\"A man stands a row of photos in front of a monument\" height=\"2220\" width=\"3828\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>A man places photos of famous victims of Soviet policy in front of the Monument to the Soviet Army in Sofia, Bulgaria in 2014.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Hristo Vladev/Pacific Press/LightRocket via Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>There is one modest monument on the island. It&#39;s an abstract stone structure, and you&#39;d have no idea what it was if you didn&#39;t already know the history. The original idea was to build a monument that listed the names of all the known internees, something like the Vietnam wall on the Mall in Washington. But the survivors and their families who pooled their resources to build it ran out of money, and no one, including the Bulgarian government, stepped in to help. (The survivors also hoped to open a museum and to recreate the shacks where they were held, but that hasn&#39;t happened either.)</p><h2><strong>My grandfather&#39;s escape </strong></h2><p>Dzhermanova, the 93-year-old anarchist - and eternal optimist, apparently - has hope that younger people will dig up the buried history.</p><p>As for my grandfather, his ex-wife (or whoever it was who betrayed him to the authorities) was right that he wanted to escape Bulgaria.</p><p>After his release from Belene in 1953, that resolve was so much stronger. &#34;After 4 years and three months in the Island of Death, I became determined to go to my real home: America,&#34; he explained in his notebooks.</p><h2><strong><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/614ce0bc4c42bc00190fa67f?format=jpeg\" alt=\"A younger woman sits at a table with two older people.\" height=\"736\" width=\"1094\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>The author with her grandfather and grandmother, Tsvetana Tutunjieva.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Tana Ganeva</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure></strong></h2><p>As he detailed it, it would take four harrowing attempts. Soon after his release from Belene, he managed to make it into Yugoslavia during a &#34;sabor&#34; - a temporary loosening of borders so family and friends in the two countries could see each other. But he got caught and was thrown into a Yugoslavian jail.</p><p>From there, he organized an inmate breakout after bribing the guard dog, Jeko, with his dinner. But he and the other prisoners were caught in the woods, and the Yugoslavian authorities gave them up to the Bulgarian authorities in exchange for 10 cows. &#34;They weren&#39;t even very good cows - scrawny,&#34; he wrote.</p><p>Several years later, he tried to cross Bulgaria&#39;s mountainous border into Greece, but he was caught once again.</p><p>Finally, he made it into Austria and then Germany by clinging to the underside of a freight train. And then on to California, where he gave his new dog a familiar name: Jeko.</p><p><em>Tana Ganeva writes about policing, prisons and criminal justice. She&#39;s currently working on a book about escapees from the Soviet bloc. </em></p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a Bets crowd are talking about this year, as several have gone through epic rallies and seen heightened volatility. </p><p>From <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/stocks/gme-stock\">GameStop</a> in January to <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/stocks/amc-stock\">AMC Entertainment</a> in June, the near 11 million-member forum has driven the conversation in so-called meme stocks that have exploded higher amid overwhelming demand from retail investors. Strong demand for stocks with shaky fundamentals has <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/news/stocks/gamestop-hedge-fund-losses-white-square-capital-closes-down-2021-6\">led to several hedge fund blowups</a> that were caught on the opposite side of the trade betting against the company in question.</p><p>GameStop&#39;s short-squeeze, in-part led by the Wall Street Bets crowd, led to <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/melvin-capital-lost-53-percent-january-after-gamestop-shares-skyrocketed-2021-1\">a more than 50% drawdown in multi-billion dollar hedge fund Melvin Capital.</a> Meanwhile, the sharp rally in struggling movie theater chain AMC Entertainment <a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/news/stocks/amc-short-seller-losses-stock-price-surge-8-day-rally-2021-6\">caused billions of dollar in losses for short-sellers in May and June.</a></p><p>As traders look to replicate the success of Wall Street Bets stocks, one data aggregator is compiling the most mentioned stocks on Reddit&#39;s forum. </p><p>These are the top 10 stocks Reddit&#39;s WallStreetBets forum is focused on right now, according to data compiled by <a href=\"https://swaggystocks.com/dashboard/wallstreetbets/ticker-sentiment\">SwaggyStocks.</a> The list is based on mentions over the past 24 hours.</p><p><strong>10. 00:55:29","updatedTime":"2021-09-28 00:55:29","title":"China bans violent or vulgar cartoons and anime as its crackdown on the entertainment industry continues","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/china-bans-violent-vulgar-cartoons-anime-entertainment-industry-crackdown-2021-9","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/6151da88b414c100186286d2?format=jpeg\" alt=\"china children\" height=\"3083\" width=\"4110\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>\n<p class=\"copyright\">GREG BAKER/Staff/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>China banned violent and vulgar children&#39;s TV shows and a popular anime show was already censored.</li>\n<li>The regulatory agency announced cartoons should promote &#34;truth, good, and beauty.&#34;</li>\n<li>China has tried to enforce morality in a broader crackdown on the entertainment industry. </li>\n<li id=\"recirc\"><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">See more stories on Insider&#39;s business page</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>In the latest sign of China&#39;s crackdown on the country&#39;s entertainment industry, Chinese authorities announced a ban on violent, vulgar, and bloody children&#39;s TV shows on Friday and sparked an uproar among social media users when a popular anime show was censored from streaming services. </p><p>The regulations apply to network shows and those hosted on streaming networks. After the ban was announced, the popular Japanese anime series &#34;Ultraman Tiga&#34; was censored from Chinese streaming services, much to the anger of Chinese fans. </p><p><a href=\"https://www.cnn.com/2021/09/27/media/china-cartoon-ban-ultraman-tiga-intl-hnk/index.html\">Though many posts about the show&#39;s ban have now been deleted</a>, the hashtag about the show&#39;s removal was viewed 84 million times, with people complaining about losing a staple of their childhood, according to CNN. The show aired from 1996 to 1997, and includes five spin-off movies. </p><p>&#34;The <a href=\"http://www.nrta.gov.cn/art/2021/9/24/art_113_58016.html\">content of broadcasts should be healthy and progressive and should promote truth, good, and beauty</a> in cartoons,&#34; a statement from the National Radio and Television Administration said. The regulatory body also said that children were the primary audience for cartoons and that streaming services should set up children&#39;s channels with child-appropriate content with shows that promote &#34;healthy development&#34; among young people. </p><p>The Chinese government has implemented a far-reaching crackdown on the entertainment industry, including <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/china-cuts-video-game-time-for-kids-3-hours-weekly-2021-8\">prohibiting children from playing video games for more than three hours</a> a week, discouraging broadcasters from casting &#34;effeminate men&#34; on TV shows, and <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/k-pop-fan-accounts-china-weibo-suspended-irrational-star-chasing-2021-9\">suspending K-pop fan accounts on social media</a>. </p><p>The government also <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/k-pop-fan-accounts-china-weibo-suspended-irrational-star-chasing-2021-9\">erased an enormously popular actress from Chinese social media</a> and deleted her movies from streaming services without explaining why.</p><p>The heightened incursions into the entertainment industry are mostly focused on promoting a specific idea of morality within film and TV.</p><p>This isn&#39;t the first time that a Chinese authority has tried to discourage the viewing of certain children&#39;s TV shows due to violence. In April, the Jiangsu Consumer Council <a href=\"https://www.scmp.com/news/people-culture/trending-china/article/3129212/barbie-dreamhouse-adventures-peppa-pig-and-my\">announced that 21 children&#39;s cartoons, including &#34;Barbie Dreamhouse Adventures&#34; and &#34;My Little Pony,&#34; weren&#39;t appropriate for kids</a>. </p><p>The state-run newspaper the Global Times hinted that half of the cartoons referenced in the investigation had &#34;violent criminal elements to varying degrees,&#34; including beatings, arson, and intimidation.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read by becoming the first transgender women to win parliamentary seats in Sunday&#39;s national election.</p><p>Tessa Ganserer and Nyke Slawik stood for the Greens party, which came third in the election, increasing its share of the vote to 14.8% from 8.9% in 2017 and is set to play a pivotal role in the building of a new three-way coalition government.</p><p>&#34;It is a historic victory for the Greens, but also for the trans-emancipatory movement and for the entire queer community,&#34; Ganserer, 44, told Reuters, adding that the results were a symbol of an open and tolerant society.</p><p>Topping the priority list for Ganserer, who was elected to Bavaria&#39;s regional parliament in 2013, is an easier procedure for ratifying a sex change on identity documents.</p><p>Ganserer, who has two sons, also wants legislative changes to allow lesbian mothers to adopt children.</p><p>Slawik, 27, said the results were unbelievable. She secured a seat in parliament through the Greens list of candidates in the western state of North Rhine-Westphalia.</p><p>&#34;Madness! I still can&#39;t quite believe it, but with this historic election result I will definitely be a member of the next Bundestag,&#34; Slawik posted on Instagram.</p><p>Slawik has called for a nationwide action plan against homophobia and transphobia, a self-determination law, and improvements to the federal anti-discrimination law.</p><p>Homosexuality was decriminalized in Germany in 1969 and same-sex marriage legalized in 2017.</p><p>But hate crimes against LGBT+ people jumped by 36% last year, according to police figures that highlight a rising trend of homophobia in parts of German
